### [253] [Uniform Diffusion Models Revisited: Leave-One-Out Denoiser and Absorbing State Reformulation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22765)
*Samson Gourevitch,Yazid Janati,Dario Shariatian,Umut Simsekli,Eric Moulines,Eric P. Xing,Alain Durmus*

Main category: cs.LG

TL;DR: The paper reveals a parameterization mismatch in Uniform Diffusion Models (UDM), showing that standard training optimizes a "leave-one-out" posterior rather than the denoising posterior, and introduces both inference improvements and an absorbing-state reformulation to bridge the gap between masked and uniform diffusion.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: There's a known discrepancy in how discrete diffusion models are parameterized and trained. While masked diffusion models (MDM) have aligned design choices, uniform diffusion models (UDM) have mismatches between their plug-in ELBO parameterization and the actual cross-entropy denoising objective, which may explain the empirical performance gap between MDM and UDM.

Method: 1) Identified that UDM's plug-in bridge parameterization is optimized by a "leave-one-out" posterior rather than the denoising posterior. 2) Derived exact conversions between denoiser, leave-one-out posterior, and score to disentangle parameterization from training objective. 3) Introduced an absorbing-state reformulation of uniform diffusion that preserves the joint law while decomposing it into masked-diffusion-like operations with simpler posteriors. 4) Implemented inference improvements including informed predictor-corrector sampler and improved temperature sampling.

Result: 1) Leave-one-out parameterizations consistently improve UDM generation in language modeling. 2) The absorbing construction matches or surpasses masked diffusion performance. 3) Inference improvements work without additional training. 4) The empirical gap between masked and uniform diffusion is shown to be driven more by parameterization and sampling design than by the marginal distributions themselves.

Conclusion: The performance gap between masked and uniform diffusion models stems primarily from parameterization and sampling design choices rather than fundamental differences in their marginal distributions. The paper provides theoretical insights, practical improvements, and a reformulation that bridges these approaches, enabling better understanding and implementation of discrete diffusion models.

Abstract: Discrete diffusion models are often trained through clean-data prediction, but the prediction can be used in different ways to define the reverse dynamics. In Masked Diffusion Models (MDM) these choices largely coincide, whereas in Uniform Diffusion Models (UDM) they do not. We show that the standard plug-in bridge parameterization for UDM is not optimized by the denoising posterior, but by a leave-one-out posterior that predicts each clean token without using its own noisy observation. This identifies a mismatch between the plug-in ELBO and the usual cross-entropy denoising objective. We characterize the leave-one-out target and derive exact conversions between the denoiser, the leave-one-out posterior, and the score. These conversions allow us to disentangle parameterization and training objective. Our results also lead to inference improvements without any additional training through an informed predictor-corrector sampler and improved temperature sampling based on the leave-one-out predictor.
  We further introduce an absorbing-state reformulation of uniform diffusion that preserves the UDM joint law while decomposing it into masked-diffusion-like sampling operations, with simpler denoising posteriors, carry-over unmasking, and a natural remasking mechanism. On language modeling, leave-one-out parameterizations consistently improve UDM generation, while the absorbing construction matches or surpasses masked diffusion. These results suggest that the empirical gap between masked and uniform diffusion is driven less by the choice of marginals themselves than by parameterization and sampling design. The code and models can be found at https://github.com/samsongourevitch/rev_udm.

*Siddhant Ray,Ganesh Ananthanarayanan,Kevin Chian,Yan Guo,Cristina St Hill,Jack W. Stokes,Victor Wang,Junchen Jiang*

  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs offer superior email labeling quality through deep contextual understanding, but using them at enterprise email volumes incurs prohibitive computational costs that hinder real-world deployment. There's a need for cost-efficient alternatives that maintain near-GPT level quality.

Method: Constructs a profiler to efficiently search the cost-quality trade-off space and identify cost-efficient alternatives to LLMs. Designs an on-demand provisioning scheme to intelligently scale with real-time load to minimize costs during peak inference.

Result: Argo achieves 148-167X inference cost reduction with negligible quality degradation and 20-640,000X lower profiling costs across 3 open-source email datasets.

Conclusion: Argo makes large-scale, context-aware email labeling practical for enterprises by achieving massive cost reductions with negligible quality degradation, solving the prohibitive cost barrier of using LLMs at enterprise email volumes.

Abstract: Email importance labeling has long been a critical yet challenging problem for businesses and individuals. Traditional approaches; such as keyword matching, user-defined rules, and sender-based heuristics; demand extensive manual feature engineering and fail to scale effectively or generalize. Recent advances in large language models (LLMs) demonstrate strong potential and a natural fit for this task, offering deep contextual understanding and superior labeling quality. However, using LLM models like GPT-4.1 at enterprise email volumes incurs prohibitive computational costs and hinders real-world deployment. We explore the trade-off space of using alternative labeling schemes as opposed to GPT4.1 scale LLMs, with the goal of achieving near GPT level labeling quality with significantly lower cost. We develop Argo, an enterprise email labeling framework, where we construct a profiler to efficiently search the cost quality trade-off space of labeling and identify cost-efficient alternatives to labeling emails. Additionally, we design an on-demand provisioning scheme to intelligently scale Argo with real time load, to minimize cost increases during peak load inference. Over 3 open-source email datasets, Argo achieves 148-167X inference cost reduction with negligible quality degradation and 20-640000X lower profiling costs, making large-scale, context-aware email labeling practical for enterprises.

### [257] [Planning, Scheduling, and Behavior in EV Charging Systems: A Critical Survey and Trilemma Framework](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.21665)
*Peiyan Xiao,Yuheng Li,Ayan Mukhopadhyay,Sai Krishna Ghanta,Sabur Baidya,Yanhai Xiong*

Main category: cs.MA

TL;DR: A comprehensive survey proposing a three-layer Planning-Scheduling-Behavior framework for EV charging infrastructure research, highlighting the "PSB trilemma" where realistic integration across layers requires sacrificing fidelity in at least one dimension.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: The rapid growth of electric vehicles is shifting focus from vehicle adoption to charging infrastructure deployment and operation. Existing research is fragmented across three interdependent layers (planning, scheduling, behavior), with cross-layer interactions often oversimplified, creating a need for a unified framework to organize and analyze this complex research space.

Method: Develops a three-layer Planning-Scheduling-Behavior (PSB) framework to organize EV charging research, analyzes literature according to decision horizon, actor objective, and coupling structure. Identifies the PSB trilemma (fidelity-tractability tradeoff) and reviews three pairwise-coupling literatures, showing how simplifications enable tractability at different costs.

Result: Shows that realistic integration across the three layers generally requires reducing fidelity in at least one layer. Review reveals that omitted layers are typically fixed exogenously or represented by static aggregate surrogates, which obscures important dynamics like long-term investment feedback, temporal grid/emissions dynamics, and heterogeneous user responses.

Conclusion: The PSB framework provides a structured way to organize EV charging research and identifies key tradeoffs. Future research should address challenges in emerging charging technologies, behavioral incentives, equity metrics, and city-scale learning-based methods that balance fidelity, interpretability, and policy relevance.

Abstract: The rapid growth of electric vehicles is shifting the main constraint on transport electrification from vehicle adoption to the deployment and operation of charging infrastructure. Charging-network design requires decisions across three interdependent layers: Planning, which determines where and how much infrastructure to build; Scheduling, which governs charging dispatch, pricing, and grid interaction; and Behavior, which captures how users choose stations, charging times, and charging durations. Existing studies have advanced each layer substantially, but the literature remains fragmented, and cross-layer interactions are often treated through simplifying assumptions. This survey develops a three-layer Planning-Scheduling-Behavior (PSB) framework to organize EV charging research according to decision horizon, actor objective, and coupling structure. We further identify a fidelity-tractability tradeoff, termed the PSB trilemma: each layer is computationally difficult in isolation, and realistic integration across layers generally requires reducing the fidelity of at least one layer. Reviewing the three pairwise-coupling literatures - Planning-Scheduling, Scheduling-Behavior, and Planning-Behavior - we show that the omitted third layer is typically fixed exogenously or represented by a static aggregate surrogate. These simplifications enable tractability but impose distinct costs: they can obscure long-term investment feedback, temporal grid and emissions dynamics, or heterogeneous user response and equity outcomes. Building on this diagnosis, we identify open challenges in emerging charging technologies, behavioral incentives, equity metrics, and city-scale learning-based methods that balance fidelity, interpretability, and policy relevance.

### [258] [Emergence of agriculture in an artificial society of reinforcement learning agents](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22256)
*Gautier Hamon,Martí Sánchez-Fibla,Clément Moulin-Frier,Ricard Solé*

Main category: cs.MA

TL;DR: Reinforcement learning agents in a dynamic environment spontaneously develop agricultural practices, revealing that individual planning, social vulnerability to cheaters, social learning as a "firewall," and emergent lock-in effects govern this major evolutionary transition.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To understand how complex collective behaviors like agriculture emerge from simple interactions, and to identify general principles underlying this major evolutionary transition using artificial societies as experimental platforms.

Method: Created an artificial society of reinforcement learning agents embedded in a dynamic ecological environment where agricultural practices emerge spontaneously without explicit instruction through coupled learning and environmental modification dynamics.

Result: Agricultural transition governed by four key ingredients: individual planning via delayed reward valuation, social vulnerability to cheaters, stabilization through social learning (acting as a "firewall" against cheaters), and emergent lock-in making agriculture irreversible once established.

Conclusion: Social learning enables propagation of successful strategies, suppresses cheater invasion, and leads to sustained population growth with nonlinear amplification of domesticated resources, revealing universal mechanisms linking individual decision-making, social interactions, and ecological feedbacks.

Abstract: The origin of agriculture represents a major evolutionary transition and a paradigmatic example of how complex collective behaviors emerge from simple interactions. Here we introduce an artificial society of reinforcement learning agents embedded in a dynamic ecological environment to identify general principles underlying this transition. Within this system, agricultural practices emerge spontaneously - without explicit instruction - through the coupled dynamics of learning and environmental modification. We show that this transition is governed by four key ingredients: individual planning through the valuation of delayed rewards, social vulnerability to cheaters, stabilization via social learning, and an emergent lock-in effect that renders agriculture effectively irreversible once established. In particular, we demonstrate that social learning acts as a "firewall" that suppresses cheater invasion and enables the propagation of successful strategies, leading to sustained population growth and nonlinear amplification of domesticated resources. Together, these results reveal universal mechanisms linking individual decision-making, social interactions, and ecological feedbacks. More broadly, they highlight the potential of artificial societies as experimental platforms to study the emergence of cultural innovations and major evolutionary transitions.

### [259] [ACCoRD: Actor-Critic Conflict Resolution with Deep learning for O-RAN xApps](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22306)
*Cezary Adamczyk,Adrian Kliks*

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Conflict mitigation is crucial for intelligent network control in Open Radio Access Networks (O-RAN). Existing solutions likely have limitations in handling control conflicts effectively, especially under varying traffic conditions.

Method: ACCoRD method uses a Conflict Resolution Agent with Artificial Neural Network trained with PPO-Clip reinforcement learning algorithm. The ANN analyzes network data and conflicting control decisions to infer optimal conflict resolution actions. Feedback from resolved conflicts is gathered to assess efficiency and adjust ANN weights during batch training.

Result: The ANN-based method improves efficiency over rule-based approaches by significantly reducing negative network events caused by conflicting control decisions in medium and high traffic scenarios. Evaluation is based on simulation data with a new proposed methodology for evaluating conflict resolution solutions.

Conclusion: ACCoRD demonstrates the effectiveness of using reinforcement learning-based ANN for conflict resolution in O-RAN Near-Real Time RIC, showing superior performance compared to traditional rule-based methods, particularly under challenging network conditions.

Abstract: Conflict Mitigation (ConMit) is a crucial part of intelligent network control in Open Radio Access Networks (O-RAN). In this paper, we propose a method named ACCoRD to resolve detected control conflicts in Near-Real Time RAN Intelligent Controller using a Conflict Resolution (CR) Agent with an Artificial Neural Network (ANN) trained with a reinforcement learning algorithm PPO-Clip. The implemented ANN analyzes data about the network and conflicting control decisions to infer optimal CR actions. The CR Agent gathers feedback from the network after each resolved conflict to assess its efficiency and adjust the ANN's weights during batch training. The evaluation of the proposed approach is based on simulation data. A new methodology for evaluating CR solutions is proposed. Results show that the proposed ANN-based method improves on the efficiency of rule-based approaches by significantly reducing negative network events caused by conflicting control decisions in medium and high traffic scenarios.

### [260] [Sibyl-AutoResearch: Autonomous Research Needs Self-Evolving Trial-and-Error Harnesses, Not Paper Generators](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22343)
*Chengcheng Wang,Qinhua Xie,Wei He,Jianyuan Guo,Shiqi Wang,Chang Xu*

Main category: cs.MA

TL;DR: Sibyl-AutoResearch introduces a self-evolving autonomous research framework with Scientific Trial-and-Error Harnesses that convert trial experiences into improved research behavior and system updates.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Current autonomous research systems lack research judgment - they convert weak evidence into prose, pilot signals into broad claims, use textual memory that doesn't learn from process failures, and don't improve from recurring failures.

Method: Introduces Scientific Trial-and-Error Harnesses that allow agents to run bounded trials and preserve outcomes. Formalizes two auditable conversion units: trial-to-behavior (linking trial signals to research actions) and trial-to-harness-behavior (linking process failures to system updates). Implemented in SIBYL system with exposed state, roles, memory, gates, and artifact traces.

Result: Retrospective audit identified eight high-confidence conversion events with median latency of one iteration. Registry shows five failure classes (duplicate results, stale numbers, unsupported statistics, etc.) were blocked, downgraded, or routed into repair. Conversion units are recoverable from realistic autonomous research workspaces.

Conclusion: The Sibyl framework demonstrates that executable workflows can be enhanced with judgment through systematic trial-and-error conversion, making conversion units recoverable from autonomous research workspaces without making comparative performance claims.

Abstract: Autonomous research systems increasingly make the scientific workflow executable: agents can propose ideas, run code, inspect results, and draft papers. But executable workflows do not by themselves produce research judgment. We analyze where current systems lose trial experience: weak evidence becomes prose, pilot signals become broad claims, memory remains textual, and recurring process failures do not change later behavior. We introduce Sibyl-AutoResearch, a self-evolving AutoResearch framework built around Scientific Trial-and-Error Harnesses. A harness lets agents run bounded trials, preserve positive and negative outcomes, and route lessons into later planning, validation, claim scope, scheduling, critique, writing, and harness repair. We formalize this through two auditable conversion units: trial-to-behavior conversion, which links trial signals to later research actions, and trial-to-harness-behavior conversion, which links recurring process failures to system updates. We implement the framework in SIBYL, a file-backed autonomous research system that exposes the state, roles, memory, gates, and artifact traces needed to inspect these conversion paths. A retrospective audit identifies eight high-confidence conversion events, with a median latency of one iteration and a maximum latency of three iterations. A recovered-failure registry further shows how five naturally occurring failure classes, including duplicate results, stale numbers, and unsupported statistics, were blocked, downgraded, or routed into later repair. These traces do not establish a comparative performance claim; they show that the proposed conversion units are recoverable from realistic autonomous-research workspaces. The SIBYL framework and system are available at https://github.com/Sibyl-Research-Team/AutoResearch-SibylSystem.

*Promise Ekpo,Angelique Taylor,Lekan Molu*

TL;DR: Modeling trauma resuscitation as a distributed game-theoretic problem to optimize healthcare worker decisions and improve patient outcomes under resource constraints.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: To design quantifiable metrics that capture healthcare worker decisions and augment current resuscitation procedures to improve patient outcomes.

Method: The method models trauma resuscitation as a distributed generalized Nash equilibrium-seeking game with coupled inequality constraints, optimized over time-varying communication graphs, incorporating clinical insights to model healthcare worker behavior.

Result: The paper develops a socio-technical formulation that facilitates optimal resuscitation outcomes considering healthcare workers' workloads, schedules, competencies, and resource limitations.

Conclusion: The paper proposes a distributed GNE-seeking game framework for trauma resuscitation that can potentially improve patient outcomes by optimizing HCW decision-making under real-world constraints.

Abstract: Trauma resuscitation is a clinical process for treating life-threatening physiological disorders in safety-critical environments, driven by the experience of healthcare workers (HCWs). Designing and optimizing quantifiable metrics that accurately capture HCW decisions may augment current resuscitation procedures with the potential to improve patient outcomes. This motivates our socio-technical formulation of trauma resuscitation as a distributed generalized Nash equilibrium (GNE)-seeking game with coupled inequality constraints. This method is optimized over a time-varying communication graph. We introduce novel insights from clinical experience to model HCWs behavior. This work facilitates the best possible resuscitation outcome given HCWs workloads, schedules, competencies, and limited resources.

### [262] [Self-Evolving Multi-Agent Systems via Decentralized Memory](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.22721)
*Guangya Hao,Yunbo Long,Zhuokai Zhao*

Main category: cs.MA

TL;DR: DecentMem introduces decentralized dual-pool memory for self-evolving LLM agents that outperforms centralized approaches in accuracy and efficiency.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Centralized memory repositories in self-evolving multi-agent systems incur communication overhead, privacy concerns, and reduce agent diversity. There is a need for decentralized alternatives.

Method: A decentralized memory framework where each agent maintains dual-pool memory (exploitation pool of past trajectories and exploration pool of LLM-generated candidates), reweighted online based on LLM-as-a-judge feedback.

Result: Theoretically guarantees global reachability and achieves O(log T) cumulative regret. Practically improves average accuracy by up to 23.8% over centralized baselines and 52.5% over no-memory baselines, while reducing token usage by up to 49% across multiple benchmarks and models.

Conclusion: DecentMem is a promising decentralized memory framework for self-evolving multi-agent systems that outperforms centralized approaches while maintaining efficiency and privacy.

Abstract: Self-evolving multi-agent systems (MAS) have emerged as a promising route to LLM agents that continually improve from experience, with persistent memory at their foundation. However, existing designs almost exclusively adopt a centralized repository shared across agents, incurring communication and coordination overhead, raising privacy concerns, and collapsing agent diversity. We propose DecentMem, a decentralized memory framework in which each agent maintains its own dual-pool memory -- an exploitation pool of consolidated past trajectories and an exploration pool of LLM-generated candidates for unseen contexts. The two pools are reweighted online based on stage-wise feedback from an LLM-as-a-judge. Theoretically, we prove that this design guarantees global reachability of the solution space and achieves $O(\log T)$ cumulative regret, matching the stochastic bandit lower bound up to constants. In practice, across three MAS frameworks (AutoGen, DyLAN, AgentNet), three Qwen3 backbones (4B/8B/14B), two Gemma4 backbones (E2B/E4B) and five benchmarks spanning math, code, QA, and embodied tasks, DecentMem improves average accuracy by up to 23.8% over the strongest centralized memory baseline and by up to 52.5% over the no-memory baseline, while reducing token usage by up to 49%.

</details>
